<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Caesar" redirects here. <p><b>Gaius Julius Caesar</b> (<small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈɡaːiʊs ˈjuːliʊs ˈkae̯sar]</a></span> and <small>English: </small><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA" lang="en-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/ˈɡaɪəs ˈdʒʊljəs ˈsiːzər/</a></span></span>; 12 July 100 BC – 15 March 44 BC), better known as <b>Julius Caesar</b>, was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_people" title="Roman people">Roman</a> general and statesman. A member of the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a>, Caesar led the Roman armies in the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a> before defeating his political rival <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">a civil war</a>, and subsequently became <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">dictator</a> from 49 BC until <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Assassination of Julius Caesar">his assassination</a> in 44 BC. He played a critical role in <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Crisis of the Roman Republic">the events that led to the demise of</a> the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.
</p><p>In 60 BC, Caesar, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Crassus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a>, an informal political alliance that dominated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_politics" title="Roman politics">Roman politics</a> for several years. Their attempts to amass power as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populares" title="Populares">Populares</a></i></span> were opposed by the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimates" title="Optimates">Optimates</a></i></span> within the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Roman Senate</a>, among them <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato the Younger</a> with the frequent support of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>. Caesar rose to become one of the most powerful politicians in the Roman Republic through a string of military victories in the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a>, completed by 51 BC, which greatly extended Roman territory. During this time he both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar's invasions of Britain">invaded Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Rhine_bridges" title="Caesar's Rhine bridges">built a bridge across the Rhine river</a>. These achievements and the support of his veteran army threatened to eclipse the standing of Pompey, who had realigned himself with the Senate after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">death of Crassus</a> in 53 BC. With the Gallic Wars concluded, the Senate ordered Caesar to step down from his military command and return to Rome. In 49 BC, Caesar openly defied the Senate's authority by <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_Rubicon" title="Crossing the Rubicon">crossing the Rubicon</a> and marching towards Rome at the head of an army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> This began <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">Caesar's civil war</a>, which he won, leaving him in a position of near unchallenged power and influence in 45 BC.
</p><p>After assuming control of government, Caesar began a program of social and governmental reforms, including the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. He gave <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">citizenship</a> to many residents of far regions of the Roman Republic. He initiated land reform and support for veterans. He centralized the bureaucracy of the Republic and was eventually proclaimed "dictator for life" (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Dictator_perpetuo" title="Dictator perpetuo">dictator perpetuo</a></i></span>). His populist and authoritarian reforms angered the elites, who began to conspire against him. On the <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">Ides of March</a> (15 March) 44 BC, Caesar was assassinated by a group of rebellious senators led by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">Brutus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Cassius</a>, who stabbed him to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> A new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_civil_wars#Late_Republic" title="Roman civil wars">series of civil wars</a> broke out and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Constitution of the Roman Republic">constitutional government of the Republic</a> was never fully restored. Caesar's great-nephew and adopted heir Octavian, later known as <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, rose to sole power after defeating his opponents in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Actium" title="War of Actium">last civil war of the Roman Republic</a>. Octavian set about solidifying his power, and the era of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> began.
</p><p>Caesar was an accomplished author and historian as well as a statesman; much of his life is known from his own accounts of his military campaigns. Other contemporary sources include the letters and speeches of Cicero and the historical writings of <a href="/wiki/Sallust" title="Sallust">Sallust</a>. Later biographies of Caesar by <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> are also important sources. Caesar is considered by many historians to be one of the greatest military commanders in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a> was subsequently adopted as a <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonym</a> for "<a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">Emperor</a>"; the title "<a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">Caesar</a>" was used throughout the Roman Empire, giving rise to modern <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a>. He has <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Cultural depictions of Julius Caesar">frequently appeared in literary and artistic works</a>, and his political philosophy, known as <a href="/wiki/Caesarism" title="Caesarism">Caesarism</a>, inspired politicians into the modern era.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Early life and career of Julius Caesar">Early life and career of Julius Caesar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2343" data-file-width="1518" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg/170px-Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg/255px-Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg/340px-Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marius_Glyptothek_Munich_319.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>, Caesar's uncle</div></div></div>
<p>Gaius Julius Caesar was born into a <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> family, the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Gens" title="Gens">gens</a> <a href="/wiki/Julia_gens" title="Julia gens">Julia</a></i></span>, which claimed descent from <a href="/wiki/Ascanius" title="Ascanius">Julus</a>, son of the legendary <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Trojan</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a>, supposedly the son of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The Julii were of <a href="/wiki/Alban_people" title="Alban people">Alban</a> origin, mentioned as one of the leading Alban houses, which settled in Rome around the mid-7th century BC, following the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Alba_Longa" title="Alba Longa">Alba Longa</a>. They were granted patrician status, along with other noble Alban families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The Julii also existed at an early period at <a href="/wiki/Bovillae" title="Bovillae">Bovillae</a>, evidenced by a very ancient inscription on an altar in the theatre of that town, which speaks of their offering sacrifices according to the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">lege Albana</i></span>, or Alban rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a></i> "Caesar" originated, according to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, with an ancestor who was born by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a> (from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> verb "to cut", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">caedere</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">caes-</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Historia Augusta</a></i></span> suggests three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymology_of_the_name_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Etymology of the name of Julius Caesar">alternative explanations</a>: that the first Caesar had a thick head of hair (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"caesaries"</i></span>); that he had bright grey eyes (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"oculis caesiis"</i></span>); or that he killed an elephant (<span title="Punic-language romanization"><i lang="xpu-Latn">"caesai"</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Moorish</a>) during the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a> in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Caesar issued coins featuring images of elephants, suggesting that he favored the latter interpretation of his name.
</p><p>Despite their ancient pedigree, the Julii Caesares were not especially politically influential, although they had enjoyed some revival of their political fortunes in the early 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Caesar's father, also called <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Caesar_(governor_of_Asia)" title="Gaius Julius Caesar (governor of Asia)">Gaius Julius Caesar</a>, governed the province of <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Asia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and his sister <a href="/wiki/Julia_(wife_of_Marius)" title="Julia (wife of Marius)">Julia</a>, Caesar's aunt, married <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>, one of the most prominent figures in the Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> His mother, <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_(mother_of_Caesar)" title="Aurelia (mother of Caesar)">Aurelia</a>, came from an influential family. Little is recorded of Caesar's childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plutsuet1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-plutsuet1-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 85 BC, Caesar's father died suddenly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> making Caesar the head of the family at the age of 16. His coming of age coincided with the civil wars of his uncle <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a> and his rival <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a>. Both sides carried out bloody purges of their political opponents whenever they were in the ascendancy. Marius and his ally <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Cinna" title="Lucius Cornelius Cinna">Lucius Cornelius Cinna</a> were in control of the city when Caesar was nominated as the new <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Flamen_Dialis" title="Flamen Dialis">flamen Dialis</a></i></span> (high priest of Jupiter),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and he was married to Cinna's daughter <a href="/wiki/Cornelia_(wife_of_Caesar)" title="Cornelia (wife of Caesar)">Cornelia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following Sulla's final victory, however, Caesar's connections to the old regime made him a target for the new one. He was stripped of his inheritance, his wife's dowry, and his priesthood, but he refused to divorce Cornelia and was instead forced to go into hiding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The threat against him was lifted by the intervention of his mother's family, which included supporters of Sulla, and the Vestal Virgins. Sulla gave in reluctantly and is said to have declared that he saw many a Marius in Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plutsuet1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-plutsuet1-16">[16]</a></sup> The loss of his priesthood had allowed him to pursue a military career, as the high priest of Jupiter was not permitted to touch a horse, sleep three nights outside his own bed or one night outside Rome, or look upon an army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar felt that it would be much safer far away from Sulla should the dictator change his mind, so he left Rome and joined the army, serving under <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Minucius_Thermus" title="Marcus Minucius Thermus">Marcus Minucius Thermus</a> in Asia and <a href="/wiki/Publius_Servilius_Vatia_Isauricus" title="Publius Servilius Vatia Isauricus">Servilius Isauricus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>. He served with distinction, winning the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Crown" title="Civic Crown">Civic Crown</a> for his part in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Mytilene_(81_BC)" title="Siege of Mytilene (81 BC)">Siege of Mytilene</a>. He went on a mission to <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a> to secure the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Nicomedes_IV_of_Bithynia" title="Nicomedes IV of Bithynia">King Nicomedes</a>'s fleet, but he spent so long at Nicomedes' court that rumours arose of an affair with the king, which Caesar vehemently denied for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hearing of Sulla's death in 78 BC, Caesar felt safe enough to return to Rome. He lacked means since his inheritance was confiscated, but he acquired a modest house in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subura" title="Subura">Subura</a>, a lower-class neighbourhood of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suet46_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-suet46-24">[24]</a></sup> He turned to legal advocacy and became known for his exceptional oratory accompanied by impassioned gestures and a high-pitched voice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and ruthless prosecution of former governors notorious for extortion and corruption.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2508" data-file-width="1645" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg/170px-Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg/255px-Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg/340px-Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulla_Glyptothek_Munich_309.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dictator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a> stripped Caesar of the priesthood.</div></div></div>
<p>On the way across the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Caesar was kidnapped by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mediterranean_piracy" title="Ancient Mediterranean piracy">pirates</a> and held prisoner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He maintained an attitude of superiority throughout his captivity. The pirates demanded a ransom of 20 <a href="/wiki/Talent_(measurement)" title="Talent (measurement)">talents</a> of silver, but he insisted that they ask for 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Caesar was relaxed and familiar with his captors, and (seemingly) joked that after his release he would raise a fleet, pursue and capture the pirates, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crucify" title="Crucify">crucify</a> them while alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> After his ransom was paid he fulfilled this promise in full, apart from one detail – as a sign of leniency, he first had their throats cut. He was soon called back into military action in Asia, raising a band of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliaries_(Roman_military)" title="Auxiliaries (Roman military)">auxiliaries</a> to repel an incursion from the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>On his return to Rome, he was elected <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">military tribune</a>, a first step in a political career. He was elected <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a></i></span> in 69 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> and during that year he delivered the <a href="/wiki/Laudatio_Iuliae_amitae" title="Laudatio Iuliae amitae">funeral oration for his aunt Julia</a>, including images of her husband Marius, unseen since the days of Sulla, in the funeral procession. His wife Cornelia also died that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Caesar went to serve his quaestorship in <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a> after his wife's funeral, in the spring or early summer of 69 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> While there, he is said to have encountered a statue of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, and realised with dissatisfaction that he was now at an age when Alexander had the world at his feet, while he had achieved comparatively little. On his return in 67 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> he married <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompeia_(wife_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Pompeia (wife of Julius Caesar)">Pompeia</a>, a granddaughter of Sulla, whom he later divorced in 61 BC after her embroilment in the <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher#Bona_Dea_scandal_and_trial_for_incestum" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Bona Dea scandal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In 65 BC, he was elected <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Aedile#Curule_aediles" title="Aedile">curule aedile</a></i></span>, and staged lavish <a href="/wiki/Ludi" title="Ludi">games</a> that won him further attention and popular support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 63 BC, he ran for election to the post of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" title="Pontifex Maximus">pontifex maximus</a></i></span>, chief priest of the Roman state religion. He ran against two powerful senators. Accusations of bribery were made by all sides. Caesar won comfortably, despite his opponents' greater experience and standing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> was consul that year, and he exposed <a href="/wiki/Catiline" title="Catiline">Catiline</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Catilinarian_conspiracy" title="Second Catilinarian conspiracy">conspiracy to seize control of the Republic</a>; several senators accused Caesar of involvement in the plot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>After serving as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a></i></span> in 62 BC, Caesar was appointed to govern <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Ulterior" title="Hispania Ulterior">Hispania Ulterior</a> (the western part of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>) as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propraetor" title="Propraetor">propraetor</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> though some sources suggest that he held proconsular powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> He was still in considerable debt and needed to satisfy his creditors before he could leave. He turned to <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a>, the richest man in Rome. Crassus paid some of Caesar's debts and acted as guarantor for others, in return for political support in his opposition to the interests of <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>. Even so, to avoid becoming a private citizen and thus open to prosecution for his debts, Caesar left for his province before his praetorship had ended. In Hispania, he conquered two local tribes and was hailed as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i></span> by his troops; he reformed the law regarding debts, and completed his governorship in high esteem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar was acclaimed <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i></span> in 60 BC (and again later in 45 BC). In the Roman Republic, this was an honorary title assumed by certain military commanders. After an especially great victory, army troops in the field would proclaim their commander <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">imperator</i></span>, an acclamation necessary for a general to apply to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumph</a>. However, Caesar also wished to stand for consul, the most senior magistracy in the Republic. If he were to celebrate a triumph, he would have to remain a soldier and stay outside the city until the ceremony, but to stand for election he would need to lay down his command and enter Rome as a private citizen. He could not do both in the time available. He asked the Senate for permission to stand <i>in absentia</i>, but Cato blocked the proposal. Faced with the choice between a triumph and the consulship, Caesar chose the consulship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consulship_and_military_campaigns">Consulship and military campaigns</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Military_campaigns_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Military campaigns of Julius Caesar">Military campaigns of Julius Caesar</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="171" data-file-width="332" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png/250px-RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png 1.5x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RSC_0022_-_transparent_background.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a> depicting Julius Caesar, dated to February–March 44 BC—the goddess <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a> is shown on the reverse, holding <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(mythology)" title="Victoria (mythology)">Victoria</a> and a scepter. Caption: CAESAR IMP. M. / L. AEMILIVS BVCA</div></div></div>
<p>In 60 BC, Caesar sought election as consul for 59 BC, along with two other candidates. The election was sordid—even <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato</a>, with his reputation for incorruptibility, is said to have resorted to bribery in favour of one of Caesar's opponents. Caesar won, along with conservative <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Calpurnius_Bibulus" title="Marcus Calpurnius Bibulus">Marcus Bibulus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar was already in <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a>' political debt, but he also made overtures to <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>. Pompey and Crassus had been at odds for a decade, so Caesar tried to reconcile them. The three of them had enough money and political influence to control public business. This informal alliance, known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a> ("rule of three men"), was cemented by the marriage of Pompey to Caesar's daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_(daughter_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Julia (daughter of Julius Caesar)">Julia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Caesar also married again, this time <a href="/wiki/Calpurnia_(wife_of_Caesar)" title="Calpurnia (wife of Caesar)">Calpurnia</a>, who was the daughter of another powerful senator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar proposed a law for redistributing public lands to the poor—by force of arms, if need be—a proposal supported by Pompey and by Crassus, making the triumvirate public. Pompey filled the city with soldiers, a move which intimidated the triumvirate's opponents. Bibulus attempted to declare the omens unfavourable and thus void the new law, but he was driven from the forum by Caesar's armed supporters. Bibulus' <a href="/wiki/Lictor" title="Lictor">lictors</a> had their <a href="/wiki/Fasces" title="Fasces">fasces</a> broken, two high magistrates accompanying him were wounded, and he had a bucket of excrement thrown over him. In fear of his life, he retired to his house for the rest of the year, issuing occasional proclamations of bad omens. These attempts proved ineffective in obstructing Caesar's legislation. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_satirists" title="Roman satirists">Roman satirists</a> ever after referred to the year as "the consulship of Julius and Caesar".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Caesar was first elected, the aristocracy tried to limit his future power by allotting the woods and pastures of Italy, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">governorship</a> of a province, as his military command duty after his year in office was over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> With the help of political allies, Caesar secured passage of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_Vatinia" title="Lex Vatinia">lex Vatinia</a></i>, granting him governorship over <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a> (northern Italy) and <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a> (northwest Balkans).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[53]</a></sup> At the instigation of Pompey and his father-in-law Piso, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transalpine_Gaul" title="Transalpine Gaul">Transalpine Gaul</a> (southern France) was added later after the untimely death of its governor, giving him command of four legions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[53]</a></sup> The term of his governorship, and thus his immunity from prosecution, was set at five years, rather than the usual one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> When his consulship ended, Caesar narrowly avoided prosecution for the irregularities of his year in office, and quickly left for his province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Gaul">Conquest of Gaul</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanRepublic40BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RomanRepublic40BC.jpg/350px-RomanRepublic40BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RomanRepublic40BC.jpg/525px-RomanRepublic40BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RomanRepublic40BC.jpg/700px-RomanRepublic40BC.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanRepublic40BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extent of the Roman Republic in 40 BC after Caesar's conquests</div></div></div>
<p>Caesar was still deeply in debt, but there was money to be made as a governor, whether by extortion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> or by military adventurism. Caesar had four legions under his command, two of his provinces bordered on unconquered territory, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> were known to be unstable. Some of Rome's Gallic allies had been defeated by their rivals at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magetobriga" title="Battle of Magetobriga">Battle of Magetobriga</a>, with the help of a contingent of <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes. The Romans feared these tribes were preparing to migrate south, closer to Italy, and that they had warlike intent. Caesar raised two new legions and defeated these tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In response to Caesar's earlier activities, the tribes in the north-east began to arm themselves. Caesar treated this as an aggressive move and, after an inconclusive engagement against the united tribes, he conquered the tribes piecemeal. Meanwhile, one of his legions began the conquest of the tribes in the far north, directly opposite <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> During the spring of 56 BC, the Triumvirs held a conference, as Rome was in turmoil and Caesar's political alliance was coming undone. The <a href="/wiki/Luca_Conference" title="Luca Conference">Luca Conference</a> renewed the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a> and extended Caesar's governorship for another five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The conquest of the north was soon completed, while a few pockets of resistance remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Caesar now had a secure base from which to launch an invasion of Britain.
</p><p>In 55 BC, Caesar repelled an incursion into Gaul by two Germanic tribes, and followed it up by building a bridge across the Rhine and making a show of force in Germanic territory, before returning and dismantling the bridge. Late that summer, having subdued two other tribes, he crossed into <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>, claiming that the Britons had aided one of his enemies the previous year, possibly the <a href="/wiki/Veneti_(Gaul)" title="Veneti (Gaul)">Veneti</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> His knowledge of Britain was poor, and although he gained a beachhead on the coast, he could not advance further. He raided out from his beachhead and destroyed some villages, then returned to Gaul for the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> He returned the following year, better prepared and with a larger force, and achieved more. He advanced inland, and established a few alliances, but poor harvests led to widespread revolt in Gaul, forcing Caesar to leave Britain for the last time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1469" data-file-width="2204" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg/280px-Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg/420px-Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg/560px-Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siege-alesia-vercingetorix-jules-cesar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vercingetorix" title="Vercingetorix">Vercingetorix</a> throws down his arms at the feet of Julius Caesar, painting by <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Royer" title="Lionel Royer">Lionel Royer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Crozatier" title="Musée Crozatier">Musée Crozatier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Le_Puy-en-Velay" title="Le Puy-en-Velay">Le Puy-en-Velay</a>, France.</div></div></div>
<p>Though the Gallic tribes were just as strong as the Romans militarily, the internal division among the Gauls guaranteed an easy victory for Caesar. <a href="/wiki/Vercingetorix" title="Vercingetorix">Vercingetorix</a>'s attempt in 52 BC to unite them against Roman invasion came too late.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_The_Roman_Conquest_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB_The_Roman_Conquest-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_first_triumvirate_and_the_conquest_of_Gaul_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_first_triumvirate_and_the_conquest_of_Gaul-66">[66]</a></sup> He proved an astute commander, defeating Caesar at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gergovia" title="Battle of Gergovia">Battle of Gergovia</a>, but Caesar's elaborate siege-works at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alesia" title="Battle of Alesia">Battle of Alesia</a> finally forced his surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Despite scattered outbreaks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_battles" title="List of Roman battles">warfare</a> the following year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Gaul was effectively conquered. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> claimed that during the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a> the army had fought against three million men (of whom one million died, and another million were enslaved), subjugated 300 tribes, and destroyed 800 cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The casualty figures are <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">disputed by modern historians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war">Civil war</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">Caesar's Civil War</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandrine_war" title="Alexandrine war">Alexandrine war</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Cleopatra_VII" title="Early life of Cleopatra VII">Early life of Cleopatra VII</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reign_of_Cleopatra_VII" title="Reign of Cleopatra VII">Reign of Cleopatra VII</a></div>
<p>While Caesar was in Britain his daughter Julia, Pompey's wife, had died in childbirth. Caesar tried to re-secure Pompey's support by offering him his great-niece in marriage, but Pompey declined. In 53 BC, Crassus was killed leading a failed <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">invasion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthia</a>. Due to uncontrolled political violence in the city, Pompey was appointed sole consul in 52 as an emergency measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> That year, a "Law of the Ten Tribunes" was passed, giving Caesar the right to stand for a consulship <i>in absentia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021248_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021248-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pompey_the_Great,_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original,_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_(22205132751).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4860" data-file-width="3147" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg/170px-Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg/255px-Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg/340px-Pompey_the_Great%2C_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original%2C_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_%2822205132751%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pompey_the_Great,_Augustean_copy_of_a_70-60_BC_original,_Venice_Museo_Archeologico_Nazionale_(22205132751).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Roman bust of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompey_the_Great" title="Pompey the Great">Pompey the Great</a> made during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> (27 BC – 14 AD), a copy of an original bust from 70 to 60 BC, <a href="/wiki/Venice_National_Archaeological_Museum" title="Venice National Archaeological Museum">Venice National Archaeological Museum</a>, Italy.</div></div></div>
<p>From the period 52 to 49 BC, trust between Caesar and Pompey disintegrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021258_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021258-73">[73]</a></sup> In 51 BC, the consul <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus_(consul_51_BC)" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus (consul 51 BC)">Marcellus</a> proposed recalling Caesar, arguing that his <i>provincia</i> (here meaning "task") – due to his victory – in Gaul was complete; the proposal was vetoed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021270_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021270-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> That year, it seemed that the conservatives around Cato in the Senate would seek to enlist Pompey to force Caesar to return from Gaul without honours or a second consulship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021273_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021273-76">[76]</a></sup> Pompey, however, at the time intended to go to Spain;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021273_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021273-76">[76]</a></sup> Cato, Bibulus, and their allies, however, were successful in winning Pompey over to take a hard line against Caesar's continued command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021272,_276,_295_(identities_of_Cato's_allies)_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021272,_276,_295_(identities_of_Cato's_allies)-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>As 50 BC progressed, fears of civil war grew; both Caesar and his opponents started building up troops in southern Gaul and northern Italy, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021291_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021291-78">[78]</a></sup> In the autumn, Cicero and others sought disarmament by both Caesar and Pompey, and on 1 December 50 BC this was formally proposed in the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021292–93_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021292–93-79">[79]</a></sup> It received overwhelming support – 370 to 22 – but was not passed when <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Claudius_Marcellus_(consul_50_BC)" title="Gaius Claudius Marcellus (consul 50 BC)">one of the consuls</a> dissolved the Senate meeting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021297_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021297-80">[80]</a></sup> At the start of 49 BC, Caesar's renewed offer that he and Pompey disarm was read to the Senate, which was rejected by the hardliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021304_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021304-81">[81]</a></sup> A later compromise given privately to Pompey was also rejected at their insistence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021306_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021306-82">[82]</a></sup> On 7 January, his supportive tribunes were driven from Rome; the Senate then declared Caesar an enemy and it issued its <i><a href="/wiki/Senatus_consultum_ultimum" title="Senatus consultum ultimum">senatus consultum ultimum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021308_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021308-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is scholarly disagreement as to the specific reasons why Caesar marched on Rome. A popular theory is that Caesar was in a position where he was forced to choose between prosecution and exile or civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Whether Caesar actually would have been prosecuted and convicted is debated. Some scholars believe the possibility of successful prosecution was extremely unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Caesar's main objectives were to secure a second consulship and a triumph. He feared that his opponents – then holding both consulships for 50 BC – would reject his candidacy or refuse to ratify an election he won.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This also was the core of his war justification: that Pompey and his allies were planning, by force if necessary (indicated in the expulsion of the tribunes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021309_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021309-88">[88]</a></sup>), to suppress the liberty of the Roman people to elect Caesar and honour his accomplishments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021320_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021320-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 10 or 11 January 49 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021322_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021322-91">[91]</a></sup> in response to the Senate's "final decree",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021331_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021331-92">[92]</a></sup> Caesar  <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_Rubicon" title="Crossing the Rubicon">crossed the Rubicon</a> – the river defining the northern boundary of Italy – with a single legion, the <a href="/wiki/Legio_XIII_Gemina" title="Legio XIII Gemina">Legio XIII Gemina</a>, and ignited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">civil war</a>. Upon crossing the Rubicon, Caesar, according to Plutarch and Suetonius, is supposed to have quoted the Athenian playwright <a href="/wiki/Menander" title="Menander">Menander</a>, in Greek, "<a href="/wiki/Alea_iacta_est" title="Alea iacta est">the die is cast</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plu65_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plu65-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>, however, notes that the more accurate Latin translation of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative mood</a> would be "<i>alea iacta esto</i>", <i>let</i> the die be cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FrijhoffSperna_Weiland1988_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FrijhoffSperna_Weiland1988-94">[94]</a></sup> Pompey and many senators fled south, believing that Caesar was marching quickly for Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021336_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021336-95">[95]</a></sup> Caesar, after capturing communication routes to Rome, paused and opened negotiations, but they fell apart amid mutual distrust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021340_(Caesar's_pause),_342_(Caesar's_offer),_343_(Pompey's_counter-offer),_345_(negotiations_collapse)_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021340_(Caesar's_pause),_342_(Caesar's_offer),_343_(Pompey's_counter-offer),_345_(negotiations_collapse)-96">[96]</a></sup> Caesar responded by advancing south, seeking to capture Pompey to force a conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021347_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorstein-Marx2021347-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pompey managed to escape Italy from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brundisium" title="Brundisium">Brundisium</a> before Caesar could capture him. Heading for <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, Caesar left Italy under the control of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>. After an astonishing 27-day march, Caesar defeated Pompey's lieutenants, then returned east, to challenge Pompey in Illyria, where, on 10 July 48 BC in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dyrrhachium_(48_BC)" title="Battle of Dyrrhachium (48 BC)">battle of Dyrrhachium</a>, Caesar barely avoided a catastrophic defeat. In an exceedingly short engagement later that year, he decisively defeated Pompey <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pharsalus" title="Battle of Pharsalus">at Pharsalus</a>, Greece, on 9 August 48 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1321" data-file-width="911" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg/170px-Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg/255px-Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg/340px-Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cleopatra_and_Caesar_by_Jean-Leon-Gerome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_and_Caesar_(painting)" title="Cleopatra and Caesar (painting)">Cleopatra and Caesar</a></i>, 1866 painting by <a href="/wiki/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me" title="Jean-Léon Gérôme">Jean-Léon Gérôme</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii,_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="662" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/250px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/375px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg/500px-Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii%2C_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_and_Cupid_from_the_House_of_Marcus_Fabius_Rufus_at_Pompeii,_most_likely_a_depiction_of_Cleopatra_VII.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This mid-1st-century-BC <a href="/wiki/Pompeian_Styles" title="Pompeian Styles">Roman wall painting</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> is probably a depiction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a> as <a href="/wiki/Venus_Genetrix_(sculpture)" title="Venus Genetrix (sculpture)">Venus Genetrix</a>, with her son <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a> as <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupid</a>. Its owner Marcus Fabius Rufus most likely ordered its concealment behind a wall in reaction to the execution of Caesarion on orders of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavian" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> in 30 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In Rome, Caesar was appointed <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">dictator</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plu37_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plu37-101">[101]</a></sup> with Antony as his <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Horse" title="Master of the Horse">Master of the Horse</a> (second in command); Caesar presided over his own election to a second consulship and then, after 11 days, resigned this dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plu37_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plu37-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dictator_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dictator-102">[102]</a></sup> Caesar then pursued Pompey to Egypt, arriving soon after the murder of the general. There, Caesar was presented with Pompey's severed head and seal-ring, receiving these with tears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> He then had Pompey's assassins put to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PluPom77_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-PluPom77-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar then became involved with an Egyptian civil war between the child pharaoh and his sister, wife, and co-regent queen, <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">Cleopatra</a>. Perhaps as a result of the pharaoh's role in Pompey's murder, Caesar sided with Cleopatra. He withstood the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Alexandria_(47_BC)" title="Siege of Alexandria (47 BC)">Siege of Alexandria</a> and later he defeated the pharaoh's forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Nile_(47_BC)" title="Battle of the Nile (47 BC)">Battle of the Nile</a> in 47 BC, installing Cleopatra as ruler. Caesar and Cleopatra celebrated their victory with a triumphal procession on the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> in the spring of 47 BC. The royal barge was accompanied by 400 additional ships, and Caesar was introduced to the luxurious lifestyle of the Egyptian pharaohs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salisbury_2001_52_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salisbury_2001_52-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar and Cleopatra were not married. Caesar continued his relationship with Cleopatra throughout his last marriage – in Roman eyes, this did not constitute adultery – and probably fathered a son called <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a>. Cleopatra visited Rome on more than one occasion, residing in Caesar's villa just outside Rome across the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salisbury_2001_52_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salisbury_2001_52-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Late in 48 BC, Caesar was again appointed dictator, with a term of one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dictator_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dictator-102">[102]</a></sup> After spending the first months of 47 BC in Egypt, Caesar went to the Middle East, where he annihilated the king of <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a>; his victory was so swift and complete that he mocked Pompey's previous victories over such poor enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> On his way to Pontus, Caesar visited <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a> from 27 to 29 May 47 BC, where he met enthusiastic support, but where, according to Cicero, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Cassius</a> was planning to kill him at this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Thence, he proceeded to Africa to deal with the remnants of Pompey's senatorial supporters. He was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Titus_Labienus" title="Titus Labienus">Titus Labienus</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ruspina" title="Battle of Ruspina">Ruspina</a> on 4 January 46 BC but recovered to gain a significant victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thapsus" title="Battle of Thapsus">Thapsus</a> on 6 April 46 BC over Cato, who then committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>After this victory, he was appointed dictator for 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Pompey's sons escaped to Hispania; Caesar gave chase and defeated the last remnants of opposition in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Munda" title="Battle of Munda">Battle of Munda</a> in March 45 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> During this time, Caesar was elected to his third and fourth terms as consul in 46 BC and 45 BC (this last time without a colleague).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictatorship_and_assassination">Dictatorship and assassination</span></h2>
<p>While he was still campaigning in Hispania, the Senate began bestowing honours on Caesar. Caesar had not proscribed his enemies, instead pardoning almost all, and there was no serious public opposition to him. Great games and celebrations were held in April to honour Caesar's victory at Munda. Plutarch writes that many Romans found the triumph held following Caesar's victory to be in poor taste, as those defeated in the civil war had not been foreigners, but instead fellow Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> On Caesar's return to Italy in September 45 BC, he filed his will, naming his grandnephew <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Gaius Octavius</a> (Octavian, later known as Augustus Caesar) as his principal heir, leaving his vast estate and property including his name. Caesar also wrote that if Octavian died before Caesar did, <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Brutus_Albinus" title="Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus">Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus</a> would be the next heir in succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In his will, he also left a substantial gift to the citizens of Rome.
</p><p>Between his crossing of the Rubicon in 49 BC, and his <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">assassination</a> in 44 BC, Caesar established a new constitution, which was intended to accomplish three separate goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_133_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_133-115">[115]</a></sup> First, he wanted to suppress all armed resistance out in the provinces, and thus bring order back to the Republic. Second, he wanted to create a strong central government in Rome. Finally, he wanted to knit together all of the provinces into a single cohesive unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_133_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_133-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first goal was accomplished when Caesar defeated Pompey and his supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_133_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_133-115">[115]</a></sup> To accomplish the other two goals, he needed to ensure that his control over the government was undisputed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_134_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_134-116">[116]</a></sup> so he assumed these powers by increasing his own authority, and by decreasing the authority of Rome's other political institutions. Finally, he enacted a series of reforms that were meant to address several long-neglected issues, the most important of which was his reform of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sue40_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sue40-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictatorship">Dictatorship</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1967" data-file-width="1261" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg/170px-Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg/255px-Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg/340px-Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caesar-Altes-Museum-Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Green_Caesar" title="Green Caesar">Green Caesar</a>, posthumous <a href="/wiki/Roman_portraiture" title="Roman portraiture">portrait</a> of the 1st century AD, <a href="/wiki/Altes_Museum" title="Altes Museum">Altes Museum</a>, Berlin</div></div></div>
<p>When Caesar returned to Rome, the Senate granted him <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">triumphs</a> for his victories, ostensibly those over Gaul, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Pharnaces_II_of_Pontus" title="Pharnaces II of Pontus">Pharnaces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Juba_I_of_Numidia" title="Juba I of Numidia">Juba</a>, rather than over his Roman opponents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When <a href="/wiki/Arsinoe_IV" title="Arsinoe IV">Arsinoe IV</a>, Egypt's former queen, was paraded in chains, the spectators admired her dignified bearing and were moved to pity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ludi" title="Ludi">Triumphal games</a> were held, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venationes" title="Venationes">beast-hunts</a> involving 400 lions, and <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiator contests</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Naumachia" title="Naumachia">naval battle</a> was held on a flooded basin at the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Field of Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Maximus" title="Circus Maximus">Circus Maximus</a>, two armies of war captives, — each of 2,000 people, 200 horses, and 20 elephants — fought to the death. Again, some bystanders complained, this time at Caesar's wasteful extravagance. A riot broke out, and stopped only when Caesar had two rioters sacrificed by the priests on the Field of Mars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the triumph, Caesar set out to pass an ambitious legislative agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup> He ordered a census be taken, which forced a reduction in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_dole" title="Grain dole">grain dole</a>, and decreed that jurors could come only from the Senate or the equestrian ranks. He passed a <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_law" title="Sumptuary law">sumptuary law</a> that restricted the purchase of certain luxuries. After this, he passed a law that rewarded families for having many children, to speed up the repopulation of Italy. Then, he outlawed professional guilds, except those of ancient foundation, since many of these were subversive political clubs. He then passed a term-limit law applicable to governors. He passed a debt-restructuring law, which ultimately eliminated about a fourth of all debts owed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Forum_of_Caesar" title="Forum of Caesar">Forum of Caesar</a>, with its <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Venus_Genetrix" title="Temple of Venus Genetrix">Temple of Venus Genetrix</a>, was then built, among many other public works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julius_Caesar,_Venus_Genetrix,_and_the_Forum_Iulium_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Julius_Caesar,_Venus_Genetrix,_and_the_Forum_Iulium-120">[120]</a></sup> Caesar also tightly regulated the purchase of state-subsidised grain and reduced the number of recipients to a fixed number, all of whom were entered into a special register.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> From 47 to 44 BC, he made plans for the distribution of land to about 15,000 of his veterans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="2016" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg/170px-Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg/255px-Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg/340px-Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roma-Statua_di_cesare.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Julius Caesar, <a href="/wiki/Via_dei_Fori_Imperiali" title="Via dei Fori Imperiali">Via dei Fori Imperiali</a>, Rome</div></div></div>
<p>The most important change, however, was his reform of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman calendar</a>. The calendar at the time was regulated by the movement of the moon. By replacing it with the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_calendar" title="Egyptian calendar">Egyptian calendar</a>, based on the sun, Roman farmers were able to use it as the basis of consistent seasonal planting from year to year. He set the length of the year to 365.25 days by adding an <a href="/wiki/Intercalation_(timekeeping)" title="Intercalation (timekeeping)">intercalary/leap day</a> at the end of February every fourth year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sue40_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sue40-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>To bring the calendar into alignment with the seasons, he decreed that three extra months be inserted into 46 BC (the ordinary intercalary month at the end of February, and two extra months after November). Thus, the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> opened on 1 January 45 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sue40_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sue40-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-4"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup> This calendar is almost identical to <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">the current Western calendar</a>.
</p><p>Shortly before his assassination, he passed a few more reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-5"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup> He appointed officials to carry out his land reforms and ordered the rebuilding of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Corinth" title="Roman Corinth">Corinth</a>. He also extended Latin rights throughout the Roman world, and then abolished the tax system and reverted to the earlier version that allowed cities to collect tribute however they wanted, rather than needing Roman intermediaries. His assassination prevented further and larger schemes, which included the construction of an unprecedented temple to Mars, a huge theatre, and a library on the scale of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-6"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also wanted to convert <a href="/wiki/Ostia_Antica" title="Ostia Antica">Ostia</a> to a major port, and cut a canal through the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Corinth" title="Isthmus of Corinth">Isthmus of Corinth</a>. Militarily, he wanted to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">Dacians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a>, and avenge the loss at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Carrhae</a>. Thus, he instituted a massive mobilisation. Shortly before his assassination, the Senate named him censor for life and <i><a href="/wiki/Pater_Patriae" title="Pater Patriae">Pater Patriae</a></i> (Father of the Country), and the month of <a href="/wiki/Quintilis" title="Quintilis">Quintilis</a> was renamed July in his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-7"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was granted further honours, which were later used to justify his assassination as a would-be divine monarch: coins were issued bearing his image and his statue was placed next to those of the kings. He was granted a golden chair in the Senate, was allowed to wear triumphal dress whenever he chose, and was offered a form of semi-official or popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">cult</a>, with Antony as his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flamen_Divi_Iulii" title="Flamen Divi Iulii">high priest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_119-8"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_reforms">Political reforms</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_reforms_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Constitutional reforms of Julius Caesar">Constitutional reforms of Julius Caesar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="747" data-file-width="934" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg/220px-La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg/330px-La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg/440px-La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_cl%C3%A9mence_de_C%C3%A9sar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>La clémence de César</i>, <a href="/wiki/Abel_de_Pujol" title="Abel de Pujol">Abel de Pujol</a>, 1808</div></div></div>
<p>The history of Caesar's political appointments is complex and uncertain. Caesar held both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Dictator" title="Roman Dictator">dictatorship</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribunate</a>, but alternated between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Consul" title="Roman Consul">consulship</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsulship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_134_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_134-116">[116]</a></sup> His powers within the state seem to have rested upon these magistracies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_134_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_134-116">[116]</a></sup> He was first appointed dictator in 49 BC, possibly to preside over elections, but resigned his dictatorship within 11 days. In 48 BC, he was reappointed dictator, only this time for an indefinite period, and in 46 BC, he was appointed dictator for 10 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 48 BC, Caesar was given permanent tribunician powers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_135_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_135-124">[124]</a></sup> which made his person sacrosanct and allowed him to veto the Senate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_135_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_135-124">[124]</a></sup> although on at least one occasion, tribunes did attempt to obstruct him. The offending tribunes in this case were brought before the Senate and divested of their office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_135_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_135-124">[124]</a></sup> This was not the first time Caesar had violated a tribune's sacrosanctity. After he had first marched on Rome in 49 BC, he forcibly opened the treasury, although a tribune had the seal placed on it. After the impeachment of the two obstructive tribunes, Caesar, perhaps unsurprisingly, faced no further opposition from other members of the Tribunician College.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_135_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_135-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Caesar returned to Rome in 47 BC, the ranks of the Senate had been severely depleted, so he used his censorial powers to appoint many new senators, which eventually raised the Senate's membership to 900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_137_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_137-125">[125]</a></sup> All the appointments were of his own partisans, which robbed the senatorial aristocracy of its prestige, and made the Senate increasingly subservient to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_138_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_138-126">[126]</a></sup> To minimise the risk that another general might attempt to challenge him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup> Caesar passed a law that subjected governors to term limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 46 BC, Caesar gave himself the title of "Prefect of the Morals", which was an office that was new only in name, as its powers were identical to those of the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_censor" title="Roman censor">censors</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_135_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_135-124">[124]</a></sup> Thus, he could hold censorial powers, while technically not subjecting himself to the same checks to which the ordinary censors were subject, and he used these powers to fill the Senate with his own partisans. He also set the precedent, which his imperial successors followed, of requiring the Senate to bestow various titles and honours upon him. He was, for example, given the title of <i>Pater Patriae</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Coins bore his likeness, and he was given the right to speak first during Senate meetings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-4"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup> Caesar then increased the number of magistrates who were elected each year, which created a large pool of experienced magistrates and allowed Caesar to reward his supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_137_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_137-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar even took steps to transform Italy into a <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> and to link more tightly the other provinces of the empire into a single cohesive unit. This process, of fusing the entire Roman Empire into a single unit, rather than maintaining it as a network of unequal principalities, would ultimately be completed by Caesar's successor, the Emperor Augustus.
</p><p>In October 45 BC, Caesar resigned his position as sole consul, and facilitated the election of two successors for the remainder of the year, which theoretically restored the ordinary consulship, since the constitution did not recognize a single consul without a colleague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_137_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_137-125">[125]</a></sup> In February 44 BC, one month before his assassination, he was appointed <a href="/wiki/Dictator_perpetuo" title="Dictator perpetuo">dictator in perpetuity</a>.
