Under Caesar, a significant amount of authority was vested in his lieutenants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-5"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup> mostly because Caesar was frequently out of Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_136_123-6"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_136-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg/300px-Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg/450px-Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gaius_Cassius_Longinus_and_Lentulus_Spinther._42_BC._AR_Denarius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">Denarius</a> (42 BC) issued by Gaius Cassius Longinus and <a href="/wiki/Publius_Cornelius_Lentulus_Spinther" title="Publius Cornelius Lentulus Spinther">Lentulus Spinther</a>, depicting the crowned head of <a href="/wiki/Libertas" title="Libertas">Liberty</a> and on the reverse a sacrificial jug and <i><a href="/wiki/Lituus" title="Lituus">lituus</a></i>, from the military mint in <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>. Caption: C. CASSI. IMP. LEIBERTAS / LENTVLVS SPINT.</div></div></div>
<p>Near the end of his life, Caesar began to prepare for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_planned_invasion_of_the_Parthian_Empire" title="Caesar's planned invasion of the Parthian Empire">war against the Parthian Empire</a>. Since his absence from Rome might limit his ability to install his own consuls, he passed a law which allowed him to appoint all magistrates, and all consuls and tribunes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_137_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_137-125">[125]</a></sup> This, in effect, transformed the magistrates from being representatives of the people to being representatives of the dictator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott,_137_125-4"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott,_137-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination">Assassination</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Assassination of Julius Caesar">Assassination of Julius Caesar</a></div>
<p>On the <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">Ides of March</a> (15 March; see <a href="/wiki/Roman_calendar" title="Roman calendar">Roman calendar</a>) of 44 BC, Caesar was due to appear at a session of the Senate. Several senators had conspired to assassinate Caesar. Mark Antony, having vaguely learned of the plot the night before from a terrified <i>liberator</i> named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servilius_Casca" title="Servilius Casca">Servilius Casca</a>, and fearing the worst, went to head Caesar off. The plotters, however, had anticipated this and, fearing that Antony would come to Caesar's aid, had arranged for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trebonius" title="Trebonius">Trebonius</a> to intercept him just as he approached the portico of the <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Pompey" title="Theatre of Pompey">Theatre of Pompey</a>, where the session was to be held, and detain him outside (Plutarch, however, assigns this action of delaying Antony to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brutus_Albinus" title="Brutus Albinus">Brutus Albinus</a>). When he heard the commotion from the Senate chamber, Antony fled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Plutarch, as Caesar arrived at the Senate, <a href="/wiki/Tillius_Cimber" title="Tillius Cimber">Tillius Cimber</a> presented him with a petition to recall his exiled brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The other conspirators crowded round to offer support. Both Plutarch and Suetonius say that Caesar waved him away, but Cimber grabbed his shoulders and pulled down Caesar's toga. Caesar then cried to Cimber, "Why, this is violence!" ("<i>Ista quidem vis est!</i>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suetonius_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-suetonius-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="1848" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg/220px-Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg/330px-Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg/440px-Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Theodor_von_Piloty_Murder_of_Caesar_1865.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">senators</a> encircle Caesar, a 19th-century interpretation of the event by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Theodor_von_Piloty" title="Carl Theodor von Piloty">Carl Theodor von Piloty</a></div></div></div>
<p>Casca simultaneously produced his dagger and made a glancing thrust at the dictator's neck. Caesar turned around quickly and caught Casca by the arm. According to Plutarch, he said in Latin, "Casca, you villain, what are you doing?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Casca, frightened, shouted, "Help, brother!" in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> ("<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀδελφέ, βοήθει</span></span>", "<i>adelphe, boethei</i>"). Within moments, the entire group, including Brutus, was striking out at the dictator. Caesar attempted to get away, but, blinded by blood, he tripped and fell; the men continued stabbing him as he lay defenceless on the lower steps of the portico. According to <a href="/wiki/Eutropius_(historian)" title="Eutropius (historian)">Eutropius</a>, around 60 men participated in the assassination. He was stabbed 23 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Suetonius, a physician later established that only one wound, the second one to his chest, had been lethal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The dictator's <a href="/wiki/Last_words_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Last words of Julius Caesar">last words</a> are not known with certainty, and are a contested subject among scholars and historians alike. Suetonius reports that others have said Caesar's last words were the Greek phrase "<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kai_su,_teknon" title="Kai su, teknon">καὶ σύ, τέκνον</a>;</span></span>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> (transliterated as "<i>Kai sy, teknon?</i>": "You too, child?" in English). However, Suetonius' own opinion was that Caesar said nothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plutarch also reports that Caesar said nothing, pulling his toga over his head when he saw Brutus among the conspirators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> The version best known in the English-speaking world is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> phrase "<i><a href="/wiki/Et_tu,_Brute%3F" title="Et tu, Brute?">Et tu, Brute?</a></i>" ("And you, Brutus?", commonly rendered as "You too, Brutus?");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> best known from Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i>, where it actually forms the first half of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macaronic" title="Macaronic">macaronic</a> line: "<i>Et tu, Brute?</i> Then fall, Caesar." This version was already popular when the play was written, as it appears in <a href="/wiki/Richard_Edes" title="Richard Edes">Richard Edes</a>'s Latin play <i>Caesar Interfectus</i> of 1582 and <i>The True Tragedie of Richarde Duke of Yorke &amp; etc.</i> of 1595, Shakespeare's source work for other plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1057" data-file-width="1798" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg/220px-Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg/330px-Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg/440px-Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_-_The_Death_of_Caesar_-_Walters_37884.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Caesar_(G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me)" title="The Death of Caesar (Gérôme)">The Death of Caesar</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-L%C3%A9on_G%C3%A9r%C3%B4me" title="Jean-Léon Gérôme">Jean-Léon Gérôme</a>, 1867</div></div></div>
<p>According to Plutarch, after the assassination, Brutus stepped forward as if to say something to his fellow senators; they, however, fled the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Brutus and his companions then marched to the Capitol while crying out to their beloved city: "People of Rome, we are once again free!" They were met with silence, as the citizens of Rome had locked themselves inside their houses as soon as the rumour of what had taken place had begun to spread. Caesar's dead body lay where it fell on the Senate floor for nearly three hours before other officials arrived to remove it.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="funeral"></span>
</p><p>Caesar's body was cremated. A crowd that had gathered at the cremation started a fire, which badly damaged the forum and neighbouring buildings. On the site of his cremation, the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Caesar" title="Temple of Caesar">Temple of Caesar</a> was erected a few years later (at the east side of the main square of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a>). Only its altar now remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> A life-size wax statue of Caesar was later erected in the forum displaying the 23 stab wounds.
</p><p>In the chaos following the death of Caesar, Antony, Octavian (later Augustus Caesar), and others fought a series of five civil wars, which would culminate in the formation of the Roman Empire.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath_of_the_assassination">Aftermath of the assassination</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M_Antonius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="487" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M_Antonius.jpg/170px-M_Antonius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M_Antonius.jpg/255px-M_Antonius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M_Antonius.jpg/340px-M_Antonius.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M_Antonius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> made during the <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavian dynasty</a> (69–96 AD)</div></div></div>
<p>The result, unforeseen by the assassins, was that Caesar's death precipitated the end of the Roman Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">middle and lower classes</a>, with whom Caesar was immensely popular and had been since before Gaul, became enraged that a small group of aristocrats had killed their champion. Antony, who had been drifting apart from Caesar, capitalised on the grief of the Roman mob and threatened to unleash them on the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimates" title="Optimates">Optimates</a></i>, perhaps with the intent of taking control of Rome himself. To his surprise and chagrin, Caesar had named his grandnephew <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Gaius Octavius</a> his sole heir, bequeathing him the immensely potent Caesar name and making him one of the wealthiest citizens in the Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="944" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg/220px-Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg/330px-Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg/440px-Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marc_Antony%27s_Oration_at_Caesar%27s_Funeral_by_George_Edward_Robertson.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Marc Antony's Oration at Caesar's Funeral</i> by George Edward Robertson</div></div></div>
<p>The crowd at the funeral boiled over, throwing dry branches, furniture, and even clothing on to Caesar's funeral pyre, causing the flames to spin out of control, seriously damaging the Forum. The mob then attacked the houses of Brutus and Cassius, where they were repelled only with considerable difficulty, ultimately providing the spark for the <a href="/wiki/Liberators%27_civil_war" title="Liberators' civil war">civil war</a>, fulfilling at least in part Antony's threat against the aristocrats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Antony did not foresee the ultimate outcome of the next series of civil wars, particularly with regard to Caesar's adopted heir. Octavian, aged only 18 when Caesar died, proved to have considerable political skills, and while Antony dealt with <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Brutus_Albinus" title="Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus">Decimus Brutus</a> in the first round of the new civil wars, Octavian consolidated his tenuous position.
</p><p>To combat Brutus and Cassius, who were massing an enormous army in Greece, Antony needed soldiers, the cash from Caesar's war chests, and the legitimacy that Caesar's name would provide for any action he took against them. With the passage of the <i>lex Titia</i> on 27 November 43 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> was officially formed, composed of Antony, Octavian, and Caesar's loyal cavalry commander <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Lepidus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> It formally <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">deified</a> Caesar as Divus Iulius in 42 BC, and Caesar Octavian henceforth became <i>Divi filius</i> ("Son of the divine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because Caesar's clemency had resulted in his murder, the Second Triumvirate reinstated the practice of <a href="/wiki/Proscription" title="Proscription">proscription</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> It engaged in the legally sanctioned killing of a large number of its opponents to secure funding for its 45 legions in the second civil war against Brutus and Cassius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Antony and Octavian defeated them at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Philippi" title="Battle of Philippi">Philippi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Statue.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="930" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Augustus_Statue.JPG/170px-Augustus_Statue.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Augustus_Statue.JPG/255px-Augustus_Statue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Augustus_Statue.JPG/340px-Augustus_Statue.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Statue.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Caesar_Octavianus" title="Gaius Julius Caesar Octavianus">Gaius Julius Caesar Octavianus</a>, Caesar's adopted heir</div></div></div>
<p>Afterward, Antony formed an alliance with Caesar's lover, Cleopatra, intending to use the fabulously wealthy Egypt as a base to dominate Rome. A third civil war broke out between Octavian on one hand and Antony and Cleopatra on the other. This final civil war, culminating in the latter's defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Actium</a> in 31 BC and <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Cleopatra" title="Death of Cleopatra">suicide in Egypt</a> in 30 BC, resulted in the permanent ascendancy of Octavian, who became the first Roman emperor, under the name Caesar Augustus, a name conveying religious, rather than political, authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>Julius Caesar had been preparing to invade <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>, and then march back to <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a> through Eastern Europe. These plans were thwarted by his assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> His successors did attempt the conquests of Parthia and Germania, but without lasting results.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deification">Deification</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divus_Julius" title="Divus Julius">Divus Julius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Comet" title="Caesar's Comet">Caesar's Comet</a></div>
<p>Julius Caesar was the first historical Roman to be officially deified. He was posthumously granted the title <i>Divus Iulius</i> (the divine/deified Julius) by decree of the Roman Senate on 1 January 42 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Comet" title="Caesar's Comet">appearance of a comet</a> during <a href="/wiki/Ludi" title="Ludi">games in his honour</a> was taken as confirmation of his divinity. Though his temple was not dedicated until after his death, he may have received divine honours during his lifetime:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> and shortly before his assassination, Antony had been appointed as his <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamen</a></i> (priest).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Both Octavian and Antony promoted the cult of<i> Divus Iulius</i>. After the death of Caesar, Octavian, as the adoptive son of Caesar, assumed the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Divi_filius" title="Divi filius">Divi filius</a></i> (Son of the Divine).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_physical_appearance">Health and physical appearance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_(Vatican_Museum).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3426" data-file-width="1890" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg/170px-Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg/255px-Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg/340px-Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_%28Vatican_Museum%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gaius_Iulius_Caesar_(Vatican_Museum).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Chiaramonti_Caesar" title="Chiaramonti Caesar">Chiaramonti Caesar</a> bust, a posthumous portrait in marble, 44–30 BC, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Pio-Clementino" title="Museo Pio-Clementino">Museo Pio-Clementino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Museums" title="Vatican Museums">Vatican Museums</a></div></div></div>
<p>Based on remarks by Plutarch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Caesar is sometimes thought to have suffered from <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>. Modern scholarship is sharply divided on the subject, and some scholars believe that he was plagued by malaria, particularly during the Sullan proscriptions of the 80s BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Other scholars contend his epileptic seizures were due to a <a href="/wiki/Neurocysticercosis" title="Neurocysticercosis">parasitic infection in the brain</a> by a tapeworm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bruschi_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-bruschi-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mclachlan_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-mclachlan-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caesar had four documented episodes of what may have been complex partial seizures. He may additionally have had <a href="/wiki/Absence_seizure" title="Absence seizure">absence seizures</a> in his youth. The earliest accounts of these seizures were made by the biographer Suetonius, who was born after Caesar died. The claim of epilepsy is countered among some medical historians by a claim of <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">hypoglycemia</a>, which can cause epileptoid seizures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes2004Caesar_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes2004Caesar-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez1995_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez1995-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epilepsiemuseumCaesar_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-epilepsiemuseumCaesar-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, psychiatrist Harbour F. Hodder published what he termed as the "Caesar Complex" theory, arguing that Caesar was a sufferer of <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe_epilepsy" title="Temporal lobe epilepsy">temporal lobe epilepsy</a> and the debilitating symptoms of the condition were a factor in Caesar's conscious decision to forgo personal safety in the days leading up to his assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>A line from Shakespeare has sometimes been taken to mean that he was deaf in one ear: "Come on my right hand, for this ear is deaf".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> No classical source mentions hearing impairment in connection with Caesar. The playwright may have been making metaphorical use of a passage in Plutarch that does not refer to deafness at all, but rather to a gesture Alexander of Macedon customarily made. By covering his ear, Alexander indicated that he had turned his attention from an accusation in order to hear the defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Francesco M. Galassi and Hutan Ashrafian suggest that Caesar's behavioral manifestations—headaches, vertigo, falls (possibly caused by muscle weakness due to nerve damage), sensory deficit, giddiness and insensibility—and syncopal episodes were the results of cerebrovascular episodes, not epilepsy. Pliny the Elder reports in his <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i> that Caesar's father and forefather died without apparent cause while putting on their shoes. These events can be more readily associated with cardiovascular complications from a stroke episode or lethal heart attack. Caesar possibly had a genetic predisposition for cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>, writing more than a century after Caesar's death, describes Caesar as "tall of stature with a fair complexion, shapely limbs, a somewhat full face, and keen black eyes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_family">Name and family</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_name_Gaius_Julius_Caesar">The name Gaius Julius Caesar</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Caesar_(name)" title="Gaius Julius Caesar (name)">Gaius Julius Caesar (name)</a></div>
<p>Using the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> of the period, which lacked the letters <i>J</i> and <i>U</i>, Caesar's name would be rendered GAIVS IVLIVS CAESAR; the form CAIVS is also attested, using the older Roman representation of <i>G</i> by <i>C</i>. The standard abbreviation was C. IVLIVS CÆSAR, reflecting the older spelling. (The letterform <i>Æ</i> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligature_(typography)" title="Ligature (typography)">ligature</a> of the letters <i>A</i> and <i>E</i>, and is often used in Latin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inscription" title="Inscription">inscriptions</a> to save space.)
</p><p>In Classical Latin, it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_spelling_and_pronunciation" title="Latin spelling and pronunciation">pronounced</a> [ˈɡaː.i.ʊs ˈjuːl.i.ʊs ˈkae̯sar]. In the days of the late Roman Republic, many historical writings were done in Greek, a language most educated Romans studied. Young wealthy Roman boys were often taught by Greek slaves and sometimes sent to Athens for advanced training, as was Caesar's principal assassin, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">Brutus</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>, during Caesar's time, his family name was written Καίσαρ (<i>Kaísar</i>), reflecting its contemporary pronunciation. Thus, his name is pronounced in a similar way to the pronunciation of the German <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a></i> (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kaɪ̯zɐ]</span>) or Dutch <i><a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">keizer</a></i> (<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kɛizɛr]</span>).
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a>, the original <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ae̯]</span> first began to be pronounced as a simple long vowel <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛː]</span>. Then, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">plosive</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span> before <a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">front vowels</a> began, due to <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalization</a>, to be pronounced as an <a href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">affricate</a>, hence renderings like <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈtʃeːsar]</span> in <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈtseːzar]</span> in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Latin_regional_pronunciation" title="Latin regional pronunciation">regional pronunciations of Latin</a>, as well as the title of <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a>. With the evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, the affricate <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ts]</span> became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">fricative</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s]</span> (thus, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈseːsar]</span>) in many regional pronunciations, including the French one, from which the modern English pronunciation is derived.
</p><p>Caesar's <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a> itself became a <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">title</a>; it was promulgated by the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, which contains the famous verse "<a href="/wiki/Render_unto_Caesar" title="Render unto Caesar">Render unto Caesar</a> the things which are Caesar's, and unto God the things that are God's". The title became, from the late first millennium, <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and (through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavic" title="Old Church Slavic">Old Church Slavic</a> <i>cěsarĭ</i>) <a href="/wiki/Tsar" title="Tsar">Tsar</a> or Czar in the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a>. The last Tsar in nominal power was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simeon_II_of_Bulgaria" title="Simeon II of Bulgaria">Simeon II of Bulgaria</a>, whose reign ended in 1946. This means that for approximately two thousand years, there was at least one head of state bearing his name. As a term for the highest ruler, the word Caesar constitutes one of the earliest, best attested and most widespread Latin loanwords in the Germanic languages, being found in the <a href="/wiki/Text_corpus" title="Text corpus">text corpora</a> of <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> (<i>keisar</i>), <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a> (<i>kēsur</i>), <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> (<i>cāsere</i>), <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> (<i>keisari</i>), <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">Old Dutch</a> (<i>keisere</i>) and (through <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a>) <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> (<i>kaisar</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Posterity">Posterity</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_family_tree" title="Julio-Claudian family tree">Julio-Claudian family tree</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png" title="Julio-Claudian family tree"><img alt="" data-file-height="1444" data-file-width="4724" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png/1000px-Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png/1500px-Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png/2000px-Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png 2x" width="1000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_families_4_Nov_08.png" title="File:Roman families 4 Nov 08.png"> </a></div>Julio-Claudian family tree</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Wives<span class="anchor" id="Wives"></span></dt></dl>
<ul><li>First marriage to <a href="/wiki/Cornelia_(wife_of_Caesar)" title="Cornelia (wife of Caesar)">Cornelia (Cinnilla)</a>, from 84 BC until her death in 69 or 68 BC</li>
<li>Second marriage to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompeia_(wife_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Pompeia (wife of Julius Caesar)">Pompeia</a>, from 67 BC until he divorced her around 61 BC over the <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher#Bona_Dea_scandal" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Bona Dea scandal</a></li>
<li>Third marriage to <a href="/wiki/Calpurnia_(wife_of_Caesar)" title="Calpurnia (wife of Caesar)">Calpurnia</a>, from 59 BC until Caesar's death</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="410" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg/220px-Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg/330px-Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denderah3_Cleopatra_Cesarion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reliefs of <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">Cleopatra</a> and her son by Julius Caesar, <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Dendera_Temple_complex" title="Dendera Temple complex">Temple of Dendera</a></div></div></div>

<dl><dt>Children</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_(daughter_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Julia (daughter of Julius Caesar)">Julia</a>, by Cornelia, born in 83 or 82 BC</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleopatra_VII" title="Cleopatra VII">Cleopatra VII</a>, born 47 BC, and killed at age 17 by Caesar's adopted son Octavianus.</li>
<li><i>Posthumously adopted</i>: <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Gaius Julius Caesar Octavianus</a>, his great-nephew by blood (grandson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_Minor_(sister_of_Caesar)" title="Julia Minor (sister of Caesar)">Julia, his sister</a>), who later became Emperor Augustus.</li></ul>
<p>Suspected Children
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus_the_Younger" title="Marcus Junius Brutus the Younger">Marcus Junius Brutus</a> (born 85 BC): The historian Plutarch notes that Caesar believed Brutus to have been his illegitimate son, as his mother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servilia_(mother_of_Marcus_Junius_Brutus)" title="Servilia (mother of Marcus Junius Brutus)">Servilia</a> had been Caesar's lover during their youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Caesar would have been 15 years old when Brutus was born.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Junia_Tertia" title="Junia Tertia">Junia Tertia</a> (born <i>ca.</i> 60s BC), the daughter of Caesar's lover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Servilia_(mother_of_Marcus_Junius_Brutus)" title="Servilia (mother of Marcus Junius Brutus)">Servilia</a> was believed by Cicero among other contemporaries, to be Caesar's natural daughter.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Brutus_Albinus" title="Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus">Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus</a> (born <i>ca.</i> 85–81 BC): On several occasions Caesar expressed how he loved Decimus Brutus like a son. This Brutus was also named an heir of Caesar in case Octavius had died before the latter. <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Syme" title="Ronald Syme">Ronald Syme</a> argued that if a Brutus was the natural son of Caesar, Decimus was more likely than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus_the_Younger" title="Marcus Junius Brutus the Younger">Marcus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Grandchildren</dt></dl>
<p>Grandchild from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_(daughter_of_Julius_Caesar)" title="Julia (daughter of Julius Caesar)">Julia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, dead at several days, unnamed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jimenez2000_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jimenez2000-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Lovers</dt></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">Cleopatra</a>, mother of <a href="/wiki/Caesarion" title="Caesarion">Caesarion</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Servilia_(mother_of_Brutus)" title="Servilia (mother of Brutus)">Servilia</a>, mother of Brutus</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euno%C3%AB_(wife_of_Bogudes)" title="Eunoë (wife of Bogudes)">Eunoë</a>, queen of <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a> and wife of <a href="/wiki/Bogud" title="Bogud">Bogudes</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rumors_of_passive_homosexuality">Rumors of passive homosexuality</span></h3>
<p>Roman society viewed the passive role during <a href="/wiki/Sexuality_in_ancient_Rome" title="Sexuality in ancient Rome">sexual activity</a>, regardless of gender, to be a sign of submission or inferiority. Indeed, Suetonius says that in Caesar's Gallic triumph, his soldiers sang that, "Caesar may have conquered the Gauls, but Nicomedes conquered Caesar."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suet.1.49_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suet.1.49-171">[171]</a></sup> According to Cicero, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Calpurnius_Bibulus" title="Marcus Calpurnius Bibulus">Bibulus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Memmius_(poet)" title="Gaius Memmius (poet)">Gaius Memmius</a>, and others (mainly Caesar's enemies), he had an affair with <a href="/wiki/Nicomedes_IV_of_Bithynia" title="Nicomedes IV of Bithynia">Nicomedes IV of Bithynia</a> early in his career. The stories were repeated, referring to Caesar as the "<a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Bithynia" title="Queen of Bithynia">Queen of Bithynia</a>", by some Roman politicians as a way to humiliate him. Caesar himself denied the accusations repeatedly throughout his lifetime, and according to <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, even under oath on one occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suet.1.2_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suet.1.2-172">[172]</a></sup> This form of slander was popular during this time in the Roman Republic to demean and discredit political opponents.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Catullus" title="Catullus">Catullus</a> wrote two poems suggesting that Caesar and his engineer <a href="/wiki/Mamurra" title="Mamurra">Mamurra</a> were lovers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> but later apologised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> charged that Octavian had earned his adoption by Caesar through sexual favors. Suetonius described Antony's accusation of an affair with Octavian as political <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slander" title="Slander">slander</a>. Octavian eventually became the first Roman Emperor as Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suet.2.68_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suet.2.68-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_works">Literary works</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4884" data-file-width="3400" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif/lossy-page1-170px-Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif/lossy-page1-255px-Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif/lossy-page1-340px-Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Julii_Caesaris_quae_exstant.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Julii Caesaris quae exstant</i> (1678)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/220px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/330px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/440px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1783 edition of <i>The Gallic Wars</i></div></div></div>
<p>During his lifetime, Caesar was regarded as one of the best orators and prose authors in Latin—even Cicero spoke highly of Caesar's rhetoric and style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Only Caesar's war commentaries have survived. A few sentences from other works are quoted by other authors. Among his lost works are <a href="/wiki/Laudatio_Iuliae_amitae" title="Laudatio Iuliae amitae">his funeral oration</a> for his paternal aunt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julia_(aunt_of_Caesar_and_wife_of_Marius)" title="Julia (aunt of Caesar and wife of Marius)">Julia</a> and his "<a href="/wiki/Anticato" title="Anticato">Anticato</a>", a document written to defame <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato</a> in response to Cicero's published praise. <a href="/wiki/Poems_by_Julius_Caesar" title="Poems by Julius Caesar">Poems by Julius Caesar</a> are also mentioned in ancient sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memoirs">Memoirs</span></h3>
<ul><li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i>, usually known in English as <i>The Gallic Wars,</i> seven books each covering one year of his campaigns in Gaul and southern Britain in the 50s BC, with the eighth book written by <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Hirtius" title="Aulus Hirtius">Aulus Hirtius</a> on the last two years.</li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Civili" title="Commentarii de Bello Civili">Commentarii de Bello Civili</a></i> (<i>The Civil War</i>), events of the Civil War from Caesar's perspective, until immediately after Pompey's death in Egypt.</li></ul>
<p>Other works historically have been attributed to Caesar, but their authorship is in doubt:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/De_Bello_Alexandrino" title="De Bello Alexandrino">De Bello Alexandrino</a></i> (<i>On the Alexandrine War</i>), campaign in Alexandria;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/De_Bello_Africo" title="De Bello Africo">De Bello Africo</a></i> (<i>On the African War</i>), campaigns in North Africa; and</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/De_Bello_Hispaniensi" title="De Bello Hispaniensi">De Bello Hispaniensi</a></i> (<i>On the Hispanic War</i>), campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.</li></ul>
<p>These narratives were written and published annually during or just after the actual campaigns, as a sort of "dispatches from the front". They were important in shaping Caesar's public image and enhancing his reputation when he was away from Rome for long periods. They may have been presented as public readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> As a model of clear and direct Latin style, <i>The Gallic Wars</i> traditionally has been studied by first- or second-year Latin students.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg/220px-Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg/330px-Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg/440px-Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Julius_Caesar%27s_crematorium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flowers placed on the remains of the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Caesar" title="Temple of Caesar">altar of Caesar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a> of Rome, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>The texts written by Caesar, an autobiography of the most important events of his public life, are the most complete <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">primary source</a> for the reconstruction of his biography. However, Caesar wrote those texts with his political career in mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>  Julius Caesar is also considered one of the first historical figures to fold his message scrolls into a concertina form, which made them easier to read. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> began a <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of personality</a> of Caesar, which described Augustus as Caesar's political heir. The modern historiography is influenced by the Octavian traditions, such as when Caesar's epoch is considered a turning point in the history of the Roman Empire. Still, historians try to filter the Octavian bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many rulers in history became interested in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">historiography of Caesar</a>. <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> wrote the scholarly work <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Histoire_de_Jules_C%C3%A9sar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Histoire de Jules César (page does not exist)">Histoire de Jules César</a></i>, which was not finished. The second volume listed previous rulers interested in the topic. <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII</a> ordered a monk to prepare a translation of the <i>Gallic Wars</i> in 1480. <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a> ordered a topographic study in France, to place The Gallic Wars in context; which created forty high-quality maps of the conflict. The contemporary Ottoman sultan <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a> catalogued the surviving editions of the <i>Commentaries</i>, and translated them to Turkish language. <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">Louis XIII</a> of France translated the first two commentaries and the last two respectively; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a> retranslated the first one afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Caesarism" title="Caesarism">Caesarism</a></div>
<p>Julius Caesar is seen as the main example of <i><a href="/wiki/Caesarism" title="Caesarism">Caesarism</a></i>, a form of political rule led by a <a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">charismatic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strongman_(politics)" title="Strongman (politics)">strongman</a> whose rule is based upon a <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of personality</a>, whose rationale is the need to rule by force, establishing a violent <a href="/wiki/Social_order" title="Social order">social order</a>, and being a regime involving prominence of the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> in the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weber,_34_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weber,_34-183">[183]</a></sup> Other people in history, such as the French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> and the Italian <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>, have defined themselves as Caesarists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Bonaparte did not focus only on Caesar's military career but also on his relation with the masses, a predecessor to <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The word is also used in a pejorative manner by critics of this type of political rule.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Depictions">Depictions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Julius_Caesar" title="Cultural depictions of Julius Caesar">Cultural depictions of Julius Caesar</a></div>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 February</span> 2021</span>. <q>... at this juncture Decimus Brutus, surnamed Albinus, who was so trusted by Caesar that he was entered in his will as his second heir, but was partner in the conspiracy of the other Brutus and Cassius, fearing that if Caesar should elude that day, their undertaking would become known, ridiculed the seers and chided Caesar for laying himself open to malicious charges on the part of the senators ...</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=University+of+Chicago&amp;rft.atitle=Life+of+Caesar&amp;rft.pages=595&amp;rft.au=Plutarch&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu%2FThayer%2Fe%2Froman%2Ftexts%2Fplutarch%2Flives%2Fcaesar%2A.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Niebuhr, vol. i. note 1240, vol. ii. note 421.</span>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, <i>Natural History</i> <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/cgi-bin/ptext?lookup=Plin.+Nat.+7.7" rel="nofollow">7.7</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080420154422/http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/cgi-bin/ptext?lookup=Plin.+Nat.+7.7" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 20 April 2008 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. The misconception that Julius Caesar himself was born by Caesarian section dates back at least to the 10th century (<i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.stoa.org/sol-bin/search.pl?db=REAL&amp;search_method=QUERY&amp;login=guest&amp;enlogin=guest&amp;user_list=LIST&amp;page_num=1&amp;field=adlerhw_gr&amp;searchstr=kappa%2C1199" rel="nofollow">kappa 1199</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130817044725/http://www.stoa.org/sol-bin/search.pl?db=REAL&amp;search_method=QUERY&amp;login=guest&amp;enlogin=guest&amp;user_list=LIST&amp;page_num=1&amp;field=adlerhw_gr&amp;searchstr=kappa%2C1199" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 17 August 2013 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>). Julius was not the first to bear the name, and in his time the procedure was only performed on dead women, while Caesar's mother <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_(mother_of_Caesar)" title="Aurelia (mother of Caesar)">Aurelia</a> lived long after he was born.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.today/20120530163202/http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Suetonius/12Caesars/Julius*.html" rel="nofollow">"Suetonius • Life of Julius Caesar"</a>. Archived from the original on 30 May 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 January</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Suetonius+%E2%80%A2+Life+of+Julius+Caesar&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpenelope.uchicago.edu%2FThayer%2FE%2FRoman%2FTexts%2FSuetonius%2F12Caesars%2FJulius%2A.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_web" title="Template:Cite web">cite web</a>}}</code>:  CS1 maint: bot: original URL status unknown (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_bot:_original_URL_status_unknown" title="Category:CS1 maint: bot: original URL status unknown">link</a>)</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Again, according to Suetonius's chronology (<i>Julius</i> <a class="external text" href="https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Suetonius/12Caesars/Julius*.html#4" rel="nofollow">4</a> <a class="external text" href="https://archive.today/20120530163202/http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Suetonius/12Caesars/Julius*.html#4" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 May 2012 at <a href="/wiki/Archive.today" title="Archive.today">archive.today</a>). Plutarch (<i>Caesar</i> <a class="external text" href="https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Plutarch/Lives/Caesar*.html#1.8" rel="nofollow">1.8–2</a> <a class="external text" href="http://webarchive.loc.gov/all/20180213130122/http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/e/roman/texts/plutarch/lives/caesar%2A.html#1.8" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 13 February 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> Web Archives) says this happened earlier, on his return from Nicomedes's court. Velleius Paterculus (<i>Roman History</i> <a class="external text" href="https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Velleius_Paterculus/2B*.html#41.3" rel="nofollow">2:41.3–42</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220731043323/https://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Velleius_Paterculus/2B%2A.html#41.3" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 31 July 2022 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>) says merely that it happened when he was a young man.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-The_first_triumvirate_and_the_conquest_of_Gaul-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-The_first_triumvirate_and_the_conquest_of_Gaul_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://global.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/88114/Julius-Caesar/9735/The-first-triumvirate-and-the-conquest-of-Gaul" rel="nofollow">"Julius Caesar: The first triumvirate and the conquest of Gaul"</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Online" title="Encyclopædia Britannica Online">Encyclopædia Britannica Online</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_(company)" title="Encyclopædia Britannica (company)">Encyclopædia Britannica</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20211205142902/https://global.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/88114/Julius-Caesar/9735/The-first-triumvirate-and-the-conquest-of-Gaul" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 5 December 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">15 February</span> 2015</span>. <q>Indeed, the Gallic cavalry was probably superior to the Roman, horseman for horseman. Rome's military superiority lay in its mastery of strategy, tactics, discipline, and military engineering. In Gaul, Rome also had the advantage of being able to deal separately with dozens of relatively small, independent, and uncooperative states. Caesar conquered these piecemeal, and the concerted attempt made by a number of them in 52 bce to shake off the Roman yoke came too late.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica+Online&amp;rft.atitle=Julius+Caesar%3A+The+first+triumvirate+and+the+conquest+of+Gaul&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fglobal.britannica.com%2FEBchecked%2Ftopic%2F88114%2FJulius-Caesar%2F9735%2FThe-first-triumvirate-and-the-conquest-of-Gaul&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFEhrhardt1995">Ehrhardt, C. T. H. R. (1995). <a class="external text" href="https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/antichthon/article/abs/crossing-the-rubicon/80ABC3DF2369F0E3035A2B68FF6B23F1" rel="nofollow">"Crossing the Rubicon"</a>. <i>Antichthon</i>. <b>29</b>: 30–41. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2FS0066477400000927" rel="nofollow">10.1017/S0066477400000927</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0066-4774" rel="nofollow">0066-4774</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:142429003" rel="nofollow">142429003</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20211121072102/https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/antichthon/article/abs/crossing-the-rubicon/80ABC3DF2369F0E3035A2B68FF6B23F1" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 21 November 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 April</span> 2022</span>. <q>Everyone knows that Caesar crossed the Rubicon because [he would have been...] put on trial, found guilty and have his political career ended... Yet over thirty years ago, Shackleton Bailey, in less than two pages of his introduction to Cicero's <i>Letters to Atticus</i>, destroyed the basis for this belief, and... no one has been able to rebuild it.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Antichthon&amp;rft.atitle=Crossing+the+Rubicon&amp;rft.volume=29&amp;rft.pages=30-41&amp;rft.date=1995&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A142429003%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.issn=0066-4774&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FS0066477400000927&amp;rft.aulast=Ehrhardt&amp;rft.aufirst=C.+T.+H.+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cambridge.org%2Fcore%2Fjournals%2Fantichthon%2Farticle%2Fabs%2Fcrossing-the-rubicon%2F80ABC3DF2369F0E3035A2B68FF6B23F1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMorstein-Marx2007">Morstein-Marx, Robert (2007). <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/25598386" rel="nofollow">"Caesar's Alleged Fear of Prosecution and His "Ratio Absentis" in the Approach to the Civil War"</a>. <i>Historia: Zeitschrift für Alte Geschichte</i>. <b>56</b> (2): 159–178. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.25162%2Fhistoria-2007-0013" rel="nofollow">10.25162/historia-2007-0013</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0018-2311" rel="nofollow">0018-2311</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/25598386" rel="nofollow">25598386</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:159090397" rel="nofollow">159090397</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210719211508/https://www.jstor.org/stable/25598386" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 19 July 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 July</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Historia%3A+Zeitschrift+f%C3%BCr+Alte+Geschichte&amp;rft.atitle=Caesar%27s+Alleged+Fear+of+Prosecution+and+His+%22Ratio+Absentis%22+in+the+Approach+to+the+Civil+War&amp;rft.volume=56&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=159-178&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.issn=0018-2311&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A159090397%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F25598386%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.25162%2Fhistoria-2007-0013&amp;rft.aulast=Morstein-Marx&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F25598386&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMorstein-Marx2021">Morstein-Marx 2021</a>, p. 262–63, explaining:
<ul><li>Any prosecution was extremely unlikely to succeed.</li>
<li>No <i>contemporary</i> source expresses dissatisfaction with an inability to prosecute.</li>
<li>No timely charges could have been brought. The possibility of conviction for irregularities during his consulship in 59 "seems to be nothing more than a pipe dream" when none of Caesar's actions in 59 were overturned. <a href="#CITEREFMorstein-Marx2021">Morstein-Marx 2021</a>, p. 624.</li>
<li>Caesar proposed giving up his command – opening himself up to prosecution – in January 49 BC as part of peace negotiations, something he would not have proposed if he were worried about a sure-fire conviction.</li></ul>

See also <a href="#CITEREFMorstein-Marx2021">Morstein-Marx 2021</a>, Appendix 2, and, contra Morstein-Marx, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFGirardet2020">Girardet, Klaus Martin (2020). <a class="external text" href="https://publikationen.sulb.uni-saarland.de/handle/20.500.11880/28669" rel="nofollow"><i>Januar 49 v. Chr.: Vorgeschichte, Rechtslage, politische Aspekte</i></a> (in German). Bonn. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.22028%2Fd291-30177" rel="nofollow">10.22028/d291-30177</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-7749-4068-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-7749-4068-0"><bdi>978-3-7749-4068-0</bdi></a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20220426190458/https://publikationen.sulb.uni-saarland.de/handle/20.500.11880/28669" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 26 April 2022<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 April</span> 2022</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Januar+49+v.+Chr.%3A+Vorgeschichte%2C+Rechtslage%2C+politische+Aspekte&amp;rft.place=Bonn&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.22028%2Fd291-30177&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-7749-4068-0&amp;rft.aulast=Girardet&amp;rft.aufirst=Klaus+Martin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpublikationen.sulb.uni-saarland.de%2Fhandle%2F20.500.11880%2F28669&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJulius+Caesar"></span></span>
</li>

"Caesar feared that the only guarantee of his rights... to stand for election in absentia under the protection of the Law of the Ten Tribunes and to receive a triumph... was his army".</span>

<li id="cite_note-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-169">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Ronald_Syme" title="Ronald Syme">Ronald Syme</a>, "Bastards in the Roman Aristocracy," pp. 323–327. Thomas Africa thought Syme had recanted this view; see "The Mask of an Assassin: A Psychohistorical Study of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus" title="Marcus Junius Brutus">M. Junius Brutus</a>," <i>Journal of Interdisciplinary History</i> 8 (1978), p. 615, note 28, referring to Syme's book <i>Sallust</i> (Berkeley, 1964), p. 134. This would appear to be a misreading, given Syme's fuller argument twenty years later in "No Son for Caesar?" <i>Historia</i> 29 (1980) 422–437, pp. 426–430 regarding the greater likelihood that Decimus would be the Brutus who was Caesar's son.</span>
